Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The Non-Necessity of Cause and Effect
Ghazali defines the Ash'ari position on causality with examples and explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that cause-effect conjunctions are not intrinsically necessary but are established by Allah's decree and custom.

Core Idea
Cause and effect are conjoined by divine custom, not by necessity.

Ghazali begins this crucial discussion by challenging the common assumption that when we see a cause, its effect must follow. He argues that the observed connection is not logically necessary but is created by Allah in a consistent pattern. This lesson presents the foundational statement and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the list of examples Ghazali gives.
2
Identify the key claim
Locate the sentence that states the core doctrine: 'ليس ضرورياً عندنا'. Understand what it means.
3
Examine the examples
Go through each example pair (thirst/drinking, satiety/eating, etc.). Why does Ghazali choose these? They are everyday observed conjunctions.
4
Understand the reason for conjunction
Read the part beginning with 'فإن اقترانها'. Here Ghazali explains that the conjunction is due to Allah's decree and creation in sequence, not necessity.
5
Reflect on the implication
Think about what it means that Allah could create satiety without eating or death without cutting the throat. How does this challenge the philosophers' view?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [١٧]
الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً، وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضرورياً عندنا ، بل كل شيئين ، ليس هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا ، ولا إثبات أحدهما وجود متضمناً لإثبات الآخر ، ولا نفيه متضمناً لنفى الآخر ؛ فليس من ضرورة أحدهما وجود الآخر ، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما ، عدم الآخر مثل الرى والشرب ، والشبع والأكل ، والاحتراق ولقاء النار ، والنور وطلوع الشمس ، والموت وجز الرقبة ، والشفاء وشرب الدواء ، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل ، وهلم جرا ، إلى كل المشاهدات ، من المقترنات في الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف
فإن اقترانها لما سبق من تقدير الله سبحانه ، بخلقها على التساوق لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه ، غير قابل للفوت (١) ، بل في المقدور خلق الشبع دون الأكل ، وخلق الموت دون جز الرقبة ، وإدامة الحياة ، مع جز الرقبة وهلم جرا إلى جميع المقترنات .
وأنكر الفلاسفة إمكانه ، وادعوا استحالته
والنظر فى هذه الأمور الخارجة عن الحصر يطول ، فلنعين مثالا واحدا ، وهو الاحتراق فى القطن مثلا ، عند ملاقاة النار ، فإنا نجوز وقوع الملاقاة بينهما دون الاحتراق ، ونجوز حدوث انقلاب القطن ، رماداً محترقاً ، دون ملاقاة النار وهم ينكرون جوازه .
وللكلام في هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات :
المقام الأول : أن يدعى الخصم ، أن فاعل الاحتراق ، هو النار فقط ،
(١) وفى نسخة « للفرق » أي الافتراق
Question [17]: The conjunction between what is customarily considered a cause and what is customarily considered an effect is not necessary according to us. Rather, any two things—neither this one is that, nor that one is this, and the affirmation of one does not entail the affirmation of the other, nor does its negation entail the negation of the other. So it is not necessary from one's existence the existence of the other, nor from one's non-existence the non-existence of the other. For example, drinking and thirst, eating and satiety, burning and contact with fire, light and sunrise, death and severing the neck, recovery and drinking medicine, loosening of the bowels and taking a laxative—and so on to all observed conjunctions in medicine, astronomy, crafts, and trades. For their conjunction is due to the prior decree of Allah—glorified be He—through His creating them in succession, not because it is necessary in itself, not subject to cessation (1). Rather, it is within possibility to create satiety without eating, to create death without severing the neck, to prolong life with the severing of the neck, and so on to all conjunctions. The philosophers denied its possibility and claimed its impossibility. The examination of these countless matters is lengthy, so let us specify a single example: burning in cotton, for instance, when meeting fire. For we allow that the meeting can occur between them without burning, and we allow the occurrence of the transformation of cotton into burnt ashes without meeting fire—but they deny its possibility. For the discussion on this question there are three positions: The first position: that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire. (1) In another copy: 'al-farq', i.e., separation.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument and a proof of the non-necessity of cause-effect connections. Ghazali begins by stating the core doctrine: the conjunction between what people customarily call a cause and what they call an effect is not necessary in itself. He explains that any two distinct things are not identical, and neither the existence of one necessarily entails the existence of the other, nor does its absence entail the absence of the other. He provides a series of common examples—thirst and drinking, satiety and eating, burning and contact with fire, etc.—to show that these pairs are observed to be conjoined, but the conjunction is due to Allah's decree and creation in sequence, not due to any intrinsic necessity. He then asserts that it is within divine power to create satiety without eating, death without severing the neck, and life even after the neck is cut, and similarly for all such pairs. After establishing the position, Ghazali notes that the philosophers (falasifa) denied this possibility and claimed it impossible. To illustrate the dispute, he selects a single example: the burning of cotton when it meets fire. He states that Muslims allow the meeting to occur without burning, or the transformation into ashes without meeting fire, while the philosophers deny both. Finally, he introduces the three positions (maqamat) into which the discussion is divided. Only the first position is given: that the opponent claims fire is the sole agent of burning. This passage thus defines the Ash'ari position on causality, provides evidence from observable phenomena, and frames the debate.

Key Terms
الاقتران
السبب
المسبب
ضروري
التقدير
التساوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali denies causality entirely, i.e., that there is no connection at all.
Correction: Ghazali only denies necessary connection; he affirms that Allah creates effects in succession according to His custom, so there is a consistent pattern.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the reason for the observed conjunction between cause and effect?
A) The inherent nature of things
B) The decree of Allah and His creating them in succession
C) Pure chance
D) The philosophers' claim

Key Takeaway
Cause-effect conjunctions are not logically necessary but are established by Allah's decree and custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If cause and effect are only conjoined by Allah's custom, not by necessity, why do you still feel anxious when your plans don't follow the usual pattern? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's direct control over every moment?
?
Consider a time when you relied entirely on a 'natural' outcome—like medicine healing or fire warming you. Did you consciously attribute the effect to Allah, or did you take it for granted? How would your heart change if you saw every effect as a direct gift from Him, even through means?
?
This doctrine means Allah could sustain your life even when all causes point to death. Are you living in a way that reflects that belief—or do you let fear of 'causes' like disease or poverty dictate your decisions? What would it mean to truly live as if Allah alone is the cause?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Philosophers' Denial and the Cotton-Fire Example
Ghazali presents the philosophers' objection and illustrates the dispute with the cotton-fire example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute between Ghazali and the philosophers over the example of cotton burning, and the Ash'ari position that the effect can be separated from the apparent cause.

Core Idea
Fire does not necessarily burn cotton; Allah may create burning without contact or prevent burning despite contact.

After laying out the general doctrine, Ghazali turns to the specific disagreement with the philosophers. He picks a vivid example: cotton coming into contact with fire. The philosophers assert that fire is the agent of burning and that the occurrence without burning is impossible. Ghazali counters that both separation scenarios are possible by divine power. This lesson focuses on that central example.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial
Read the Arabic text starting from 'وأنكر الفلاسفة'. Notice that the philosophers claimed impossibility (استحالته).
2
Focus on the example
Ghazali selects one example: cotton meeting fire. Identify the two scenarios he allows: meeting without burning, and burning without meeting.
3
Contrast the positions
Compare what Ghazali allows with what the philosophers deny. Why do they think it's impossible? (Ghazali doesn't give their argument here, but the lesson can note that they believed in necessary causal connection.)
4
Reflect on the implications
If fire does not necessarily burn, what does this imply about natural laws and miracles? How does this support the Ash'ari view of divine omnipotence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [١٧]
الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً، وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضرورياً عندنا ، بل كل شيئين ، ليس هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا ، ولا إثبات أحدهما وجود متضمناً لإثبات الآخر ، ولا نفيه متضمناً لنفى الآخر ؛ فليس من ضرورة أحدهما وجود الآخر ، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما ، عدم الآخر مثل الرى والشرب ، والشبع والأكل ، والاحتراق ولقاء النار ، والنور وطلوع الشمس ، والموت وجز الرقبة ، والشفاء وشرب الدواء ، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل ، وهلم جرا ، إلى كل المشاهدات ، من المقترنات في الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fire has an inherent power to burn that cannot be separated from its effect.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali allow regarding cotton and fire?
A) Only that fire always burns cotton
B) That meeting can occur without burning and burning can occur without meeting
C) That neither scenario is possible
D) That the philosophers are correct

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the cotton-fire example to show that the effect can be separated from its apparent cause by divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often trust the natural patterns of your life. But are you attributing power to the means rather than Allah? In what area of your life do you act as if fire must burn, rather than that Allah alone creates burning?
?
When something unexpected happens, do you credit 'luck' or 'coincidence' instead of seeing Allah's direct action? How would your daily gratitude change if you truly believed that cotton can burn without fire?
?
The philosophers denied this because they only trusted observation. Where in your faith do you rely on what you see more than what Allah has revealed about His absolute power? What would it mean for you to wake up tomorrow and live as if every effect is directly from Allah?

The Three Positions on Causality – The First Position
Ghazali introduces the three-part structure of the debate and states the first position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of the three positions (maqamat) Ghazali outlines in the debate on causality: the claim that fire is the sole agent of burning.

Core Idea
The first position is that fire alone causes burning, a view Ghazali will refute.

Ghazali announces that the discussion on causality involves three positions (maqamat). He begins with the first: the opponent claims that fire is the sole agent (fa'il) of burning. This lesson presents the opening of that positional framework and sets the stage for the subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the announcement
Read the Arabic text from 'وللكلام في هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات'. Note that there are three positions to be discussed.
2
Identify the first position
The first position is that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire. Understand the claim: fire is the independent cause.
3
Understand the context
This is setting up the debate. The subsequent positions (not given in this passage) will involve divine agency. For now, focus on this claim.
4
Reflect on the implication
If fire is the sole agent, then what role does Allah play? How does this contradict Ghazali's earlier statement?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [١٧]
الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً، وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضرورياً عندنا ، بل كل شيئين ، ليس هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا ، ولا إثبات أحدهما وجود متضمناً لإثبات الآخر ، ولا نفيه متضمناً لنفى الآخر ؛ فليس من ضرورة أحدهما وجود الآخر ، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما ، عدم الآخر مثل الرى والشرب ، والشبع والأكل ، والاحتراق ولقاء النار ، والنور وطلوع الشمس ، والموت وجز الرقبة ، والشفاء وشرب الدواء ، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل ، وهلم جرا ، إلى كل المشاهدات ، من المقترنات في الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف
فإن اقترانها لما سبق من تقدير الله سبحانه ، بخلقها على التساوق لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه ، غير قابل للفوت (١) ، بل في المقدور خلق الشبع دون الأكل ، وخلق الموت دون جز الرقبة ، وإدامة الحياة ، مع جز الرقبة وهلم جرا إلى جميع المقترنات .
وأنكر الفلاسفة إمكانه ، وادعوا استحالته
والنظر فى هذه الأمور الخارجة عن الحصر يطول ، فلنعين مثالا واحدا ، وهو الاحتراق فى القطن مثلا ، عند ملاقاة النار ، فإنا نجوز وقوع الملاقاة بينهما دون الاحتراق ، ونجوز حدوث انقلاب القطن ، رماداً محترقاً ، دون ملاقاة النار وهم ينكرون جوازه .
وللكلام في هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات :
المقام الأول : أن يدعى الخصم ، أن فاعل الاحتراق ، هو النار فقط ،
(١) وفى نسخة « للفرق » أي الافتراق
Question [17]: The conjunction between what is customarily considered a cause and what is customarily considered an effect is not necessary according to us. Rather, any two things—neither this one is that, nor that one is this, and the affirmation of one does not entail the affirmation of the other, nor does its negation entail the negation of the other. So it is not necessary from one's existence the existence of the other, nor from one's non-existence the non-existence of the other. For example, drinking and thirst, eating and satiety, burning and contact with fire, light and sunrise, death and severing the neck, recovery and drinking medicine, loosening of the bowels and taking a laxative—and so on to all observed conjunctions in medicine, astronomy, crafts, and trades. For their conjunction is due to the prior decree of Allah—glorified be He—through His creating them in succession, not because it is necessary in itself, not subject to cessation (1). Rather, it is within possibility to create satiety without eating, to create death without severing the neck, to prolong life with the severing of the neck, and so on to all conjunctions. The philosophers denied its possibility and claimed its impossibility. The examination of these countless matters is lengthy, so let us specify a single example: burning in cotton, for instance, when meeting fire. For we allow that the meeting can occur between them without burning, and we allow the occurrence of the transformation of cotton into burnt ashes without meeting fire—but they deny its possibility. For the discussion on this question there are three positions: The first position: that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire. (1) In another copy: 'al-farq', i.e., separation.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument and a proof of the non-necessity of cause-effect connections. Ghazali begins by stating the core doctrine: the conjunction between what people customarily call a cause and what they call an effect is not necessary in itself. He explains that any two distinct things are not identical, and neither the existence of one necessarily entails the existence of the other, nor does its absence entail the absence of the other. He provides a series of common examples—thirst and drinking, satiety and eating, burning and contact with fire, etc.—to show that these pairs are observed to be conjoined, but the conjunction is due to Allah's decree and creation in sequence, not due to any intrinsic necessity. He then asserts that it is within divine power to create satiety without eating, death without severing the neck, and life even after the neck is cut, and similarly for all such pairs. After establishing the position, Ghazali notes that the philosophers (falasifa) denied this possibility and claimed it impossible. To illustrate the dispute, he selects a single example: the burning of cotton when it meets fire. He states that Muslims allow the meeting to occur without burning, or the transformation into ashes without meeting fire, while the philosophers deny both. Finally, he introduces the three positions (maqamat) into which the discussion is divided. Only the first position is given: that the opponent claims fire is the sole agent of burning. This passage thus defines the Ash'ari position on causality, provides evidence from observable phenomena, and frames the debate.

الخصم
فاعل
النار

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first position is Ghazali's own view.
Correction: The first position is that of the philosophers; Ghazali is presenting their claim before refuting it.

Checkpoint
According to the first position, who or what is the agent of burning?
A) Allah
B) Fire
C) The cotton
D) Both fire and Allah

Key Takeaway
The first position in the debate asserts that fire is the sole agent of burning, a view Ghazali rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the first position were true, what would that imply about Allah's power over the most ordinary events? How does that affect your feeling of dependence on Him in your daily life?
?
You have just learned that Ghazali frames this as a debate. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold about how things work that you have never questioned? Choose one belief and commit to examining it in light of tawheed this week.

The Problem of Causal Necessity
Ghazali states the problem of causal necessity and gives the first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the necessary causal connection and his argument that observed conjunctions are due to God's decree rather than intrinsic necessity.

Core Idea
Causal necessity is an illusion; only God's will creates the habitual sequence.

Ghazali opens the seventeenth problem of the Tahafut by challenging the foundational assumption of Aristotelian science: the necessary connection between cause and effect. He argues that what we observe is merely a habit established by God, who is the true agent behind every event. This lesson introduces Ghazali's general principle and the first stage of his argument against the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify Ghazali's main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Ghazali states that the conjunction between cause and effect is not necessary.
He uses the phrase 'ليس ضرورياً عندنا' to assert this.
2
Examine the examples and their implications
Look at the examples: drinking and quenched thirst, eating and satiety, fire and burning. What do they demonstrate about causation?
Ghazali says the conjunction is due to God's decree, not intrinsic necessity.
3
Focus on the burning cotton example
Ghazali narrows the discussion to the burning of cotton upon contact with fire. Note the two possibilities he allows: contact without burning, or burning without contact.
This illustrates that God could create the effect without the usual cause.
4
Analyze the first stage of the dispute
The philosopher claims fire is the agent by nature; Ghazali denies this, stating God is the true agent. Find the proof Ghazali demands and his critique of observation-based evidence.
Observation shows temporal succession, not causal production.

الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً، وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضرورياً عندنا ، بل كل شيئين ، ليس هذا ذاك ، ولا ذاك هذا ، ولا إثبات أحدهما متضمناً لإثبات الآخر ، ولا نفيه متضمناً لنفى الآخر ؛ فليس من ضرورة أحدهما وجود الآخر ، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما ، عدم الآخر مثل الرى والشرب ، والشبع والأكل ، والاحتراق ولقاء النار ، والنور وطلوع الشمس ، والموت وجز الرقبة ، والشفاء وشرب الدواء ، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل ، وهلم جرا ، إلى كل المشاهدات ، من المقترنات في الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف .

وللكلام في هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات :
المقام الأول : أن يدعى الخصم ، أن فاعل الاحتراق ، هو النار فقط ، وهو فاعل بالطبع لا بالاختيار ، فلا يمكنه الكف عما هو في طبعه ، بعد ملاقاته لمحل قابل له . وهذا مما ننكره ، بل نقول : فاعل الاحتراق ، بخلق السواد في القطن ، والتفرق فى أجزائه ، وجعله حراقاً ، أو رماداً ، هو الله تعالى ، إما بوساطة الملائكة ، أو بغير وساطة ، فأما النار وهي جماد ، فلا فعل لها .
فما الدليل على أنها الفاعل ؟ ! وليس لهم دليل ، إلا مشاهدة حصول الاحتراق منذ ملاقاة النار ، والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عندها ، ولا تدل على الحصول ها
Problem [17]: The conjunction between what is habitually believed to be a cause and what is believed to be an effect is not necessary for us. Rather, any two things, neither of which is that nor that, and the affirmation of one does not entail the affirmation of the other, nor does its negation entail the negation of the other; it is not necessary that if one exists the other exists, nor is it necessary that if one does not exist the other does not exist—like quenching thirst and drinking, satiety and eating, burning and contact with fire, light and sunrise, death and severing the neck, recovery and drinking medicine, loosening of the bowels and using a laxative, and so on, in all observed conjunctions in medicine, astronomy, crafts, and trades. For their conjunction is due to the prior decree of God (subhanahu) creating them in succession, not because it is necessary in itself and cannot be otherwise; rather, it is within [divine] power to create satiety without eating, to create death without severing the neck, to sustain life despite severing the neck, and so on, in all conjunctions. The philosophers denied this possibility and claimed its impossibility. The discussion of these countless matters would be lengthy, so let us take a single example, namely, the burning of cotton, for instance, upon contact with fire. For we allow that contact can occur between them without burning, and we allow that the cotton can be turned into burnt ashes without contact with fire, but they deny its permissibility. The discussion of this problem has three stages: The first stage: that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire, and it acts by nature, not by choice, so it cannot refrain from what is in its nature after encountering a receptive substrate. This is what we deny. Rather, we say: The agent of burning—by creating blackness in the cotton, causing its parts to separate, and making it cinder or ashes—is God the Exalted, either through the mediation of angels or without mediation. As for fire, which is inanimate, it has no action. So what proof is there that it is the agent? They have no proof except the observation of the occurrence of burning upon contact with fire. Observation indicates that it occurs at the time of contact, but it does not indicate that it occurs from it.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a definition and first argument of the seventeenth problem of the Tahafut, concerning the necessary connection between cause and effect. Ghazali begins by stating that the conjunction between what is habitually taken as a cause and its effect is not necessary. He explains that for any two distinct things, affirming one does not entail affirming the other, and negating one does not entail negating the other. He gives everyday examples: drinking and quenching thirst, eating and satiety, fire and burning, sunrise and light, etc. The observed conjunction is due to God's prior decree creating them in succession, not because the connection is intrinsically necessary. God can create the effect without the usual cause or vice versa. The philosophers deny this possibility. Ghazali then focuses on the example of burning cotton upon contact with fire. He outlines three stages of discussion, but here only the first stage is presented. In this stage, the opponent (the philosopher) claims that fire alone is the agent of burning, acting by nature without choice, so it cannot refrain from burning when it meets combustible matter. Ghazali refutes this: the true agent is God, who creates the blackening, separation, and transformation of the cotton into cinder or ash. God may act through angels or directly. Fire is inanimate and has no action. Ghazali then demands proof that fire is the agent. The only evidence they have is the observation that burning occurs at the time of contact. But observation only shows temporal concomitance, not causal necessity. Thus, the philosophical claim of necessary causation is unfounded.

فاعل بالاختيار
مشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies that fire regularly burns cotton; he denies the necessity and inherent causal power of fire.
Correction: Ghazali affirms the regular occurrence but attributes it to God's habitual creation, not to an intrinsic necessity in fire.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali in this passage, what is the basis for the observed conjunction between fire and burning?
A) The fire has an intrinsic nature that necessitates burning
B) God's prior decree creating them in succession
C) The natural laws of physics that cannot be broken
D) The independent power of the fire

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that causal necessity is false; observed regularities are simply God's habitual way of creating events in succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you drink water and your thirst is quenched, do you deeply feel that God directly created that satisfaction, or do you take it as a mere natural process? What would change in your heart if you truly lived with the awareness that every effect is God's direct creation?
?
If you genuinely believed that fire does not burn by itself but only at God's command, would you still fear the fire as much, or would your reliance shift to God alone? What material 'causes' in your life do you trust more than you trust the Creator behind them?
?
Ghazali shows that observation only proves conjunction, not causation. Think of a belief you hold about cause and effect in your daily life (e.g., 'I study hard so I will succeed'). Is that belief based on evidence of necessity, or just habit? How could this lesson free you from placing absolute trust in worldly means?

Ghazali's Refutation of Natural Causality in Fire
Ghazali argues that fire does not cause burning; God creates the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that observed conjunction does not prove necessary causation, and that God is the true agent of all effects.

Core Idea
Fire does not cause burning; God creates the effect concurrent with fire.

In this passage from the Tahafut al-Falasifa, Imam al-Ghazali takes on the philosophers' claim that natural agents act necessarily. He argues that mere observation of sequence cannot establish necessary causal connection, using philosophical premises and a vivid example to show that the true agent is always God.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the opposing view
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the philosophers' claim that fire is an agent by nature and cannot refrain from burning. Note Ghazali's immediate denial.
The phrase 'فاعل بالطبع' means 'agent by nature'.
2
Analyze the 'at' vs 'by' distinction
Identify Ghazali's key argument: observation shows occurrence 'at' fire but not 'by' fire. Understand why this distinction is crucial for refuting necessary causation.
3
Examine the analogy from life and faculties
See how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own admissions (that life and faculties are not from physical natures) to undermine their position on fire.
This shows that even philosophers accept coexistence without causation.
4
Work through the blind man example
Study the example of the blind man who mistakes the opening of his eyes as the cause of sight. Understand how it illustrates the principle that conjunction does not prove causation.
The blind man's error is similar to the philosophers' error about fire.
5
Synthesize the main point
Summarize that Ghazali denies necessary natural causality while affirming that God is the direct cause of all events. Reflect on how this argument fits into his broader project.
This lesson is foundational for understanding occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو فاعل بالطبع لا بالاختيار، فلا يمكنه الكف عما هو في طبعه، بعد ملاقاته لمحل قابل له. وهذا مما ننكره، بل نقول: فاعل الاحتراق، بخلق السواد في القطن، والتفرق في أجزائه، وجعله حراقاً أو رماداً، هو الله تعالى، إما بوساطة الملائكة، أو بغير وساطة، فأما النار وهي جماد، فلا فعل لها.

فما الدليل على أنها الفاعل؟! وليس لهم دليل، إلا مشاهدة حصول الاحتراق عند ملاقاة النار، والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عندها، ولا تدل على الحصول به، وأنه لا علة له سواها، إذ لا خلاف في أن انسلاك الروح والقوى المدركة والمحركة، في نطفة الحيوانات، ليس يتولد عن الطبائع المحصورة في الحرارة والبرودة، والرطوبة واليبوسة، ولا أن الأب فاعل ابنه، بإيداع النطفة في الرحم، ولا هو فاعل حياته، وبصره، وسمعه، وسائر المعاني التي هي فيه، ومعلوم أنها موجودة عنده، ولم يقل أحد، إنها موجودة به، بل وجودها من جهة الأول، إما بغير واسطة، وإما بواسطة الملائكة، الموكلين بهذه الأمور الحادثة. وهذا مما يقطع به الفلاسفة القائلون بالصانع، والكلام معهم.

فقد تبين أن الوجود عند الشيء، لا يدل على أنه موجود به.

بل نبين هذا بمثال، وهو أن الأكمه، لو كانت في عينيه غشاوة، ولم يسمع من الناس الفرق بين الليل والنهار، لو انكشفت الغشاوة عن عينيه نهاراً وفتح أجفانه، فرأى الألوان، ظن أن الإدراك الحاصل في عينيه لصور الألوان، فاعله فتح البصر، وأنه مهما كان بصره سليماً ومفتوحاً، والحجاب
And it (fire) is an agent by nature, not by choice, so it cannot refrain from what is in its nature after encountering a receptive subject. And this we deny. Rather we say: The agent of burning—by creating blackness in the cotton, and disintegration in its parts, and making it a cinder or ash—is God Most High, either through the mediation of angels or without mediation. As for fire, which is inanimate, it has no action.

What proof is there that it is the agent? They have no proof except observing the occurrence of burning at the time of contact with fire. Observation indicates occurrence alongside it, but does not indicate occurrence by it, and that there is no cause other than it. For there is no dispute that the insertion of the spirit and the perceptive and motive powers in the sperm of animals is not generated from the natures confined to heat, cold, moisture, and dryness; nor that the father is the agent of his son by depositing the sperm in the womb; nor is he the agent of his life, sight, hearing, and all the meanings that are in him. It is known that they exist in conjunction with him, yet no one says they exist by him. Rather their existence is from the First, either without intermediary or through the angels entrusted with these generated things. This is something affirmed by the philosophers who affirm the Maker, and the discourse is with them.

It has become clear that existence alongside a thing does not indicate that it exists by it.

Rather we demonstrate this with an example: A blind man who had a covering over his eyes and had not heard from people the difference between night and day—if the covering were removed from his eyes in the daytime and he opened his eyelids and saw colors, he would suppose that the perception occurring in his eyes of the forms of colors had as its agent the opening of the sight, and that whenever his sight is sound and open, and the veil...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that natural agents act by nature and necessarily produce their effects. Ghazali begins by stating the opposing view: that fire is an agent by nature, not by choice, and thus cannot refrain from burning when it meets a combustible object. He immediately denies this, asserting instead that the true agent of burning is God, who creates the blackness, disintegration, and ash either directly or through angels. Fire itself, being inanimate, has no action.

He then challenges the evidence for fire being the agent: the only proof offered is the observed conjunction of fire and burning. But mere observation of occurrence 'at the time of' contact does not prove occurrence 'by' it, nor that no other cause exists. He draws an analogy from the philosophers' own accepted principles: the generation of life and faculties in the animal sperm is not attributed to the physical natures (heat, cold, etc.), nor does anyone claim that the father is the agent of his son's life, sight, etc. These are known to be from God. Thus the philosophers themselves admit that coexistence does not imply causation in these cases.

Ghazali then states the general principle: 'Existence alongside a thing does not indicate that it exists by it.' He illustrates with the example of a blind man who, having never seen light and darkness, mistakes the opening of his eyes as the cause of seeing. In reality, the opening is merely a condition, not the agent. This reinforces the point that contingent conjunction does not prove necessary causation.

The argument is presented in the context of debating philosophers who affirm a Creator; Ghazali uses their own admissions against them to show that causality is not necessary but rather depends on God's direct action.

Key Terms
فاعل بالطبع
الفاعل
العلة
الوجود عند الشيء
الأكمه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies all regularity in nature.
Correction: Ghazali denies necessary causal efficacy but affirms that God creates effects in a regular pattern; the regularity is not necessary but contingent on God's will.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main objection to the claim that fire is the agent of burning?
A) Fire is too weak to cause burning
B) Observation shows burning occurs with fire (at it), but does not prove it occurs by fire (by it)
C) Fire does not exist
D) The philosophers agree fire is not an agent

Key Takeaway
Observing that two events occur together does not prove that one causes the other; God is the sole agent of all events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a natural event like rain causing crops to grow, does your heart instinctively give thanks to the rain or to the Creator who brings it about? What would it take for you to see every event as a direct act of God, not just a natural process?
?
The blind man thought that opening his eyelids caused sight. In your own life, what success or blessing do you attribute to your own effort, skill, or the means you used, forgetting that God is the ultimate agent? Will you take a moment today to mentally attribute a recent achievement solely to Him?
?
Ghazali argues that the father is not the agent of his son's life, even though life appears at his begetting. When you perform an action—like giving charity or teaching—do you internally feel that your action causes the good outcome, or do you recognize that God creates the effect concurrently? How can you train your soul to feel constant dependence on God's creative power in every moment?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Existence with something is not existence by it
Ghazali argues that nearness does not imply causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between existence with a thing and existence by it, and why mere proximity does not prove causation.

Core Idea
Existence with a thing does not prove existence by that thing.

This passage from the Tahafut al-Falasifa addresses a key fallacy: confusing spatial or temporal proximity with true causation. Ghazali uses the example of a father and child to show that effects like life and sight are not produced by the father but come directly from Allah. The discussion refutes those who mistakenly attribute causal power to mere intermediaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the key terms: القوى المدركة, القوى المحركة.
2
Identify the main examples
Notice Ghazali uses two examples: the powers in animal sperm not arising from the four elements, and the father not being the agent of his son's life and senses.
These illustrate that observable nearness doesn't imply causal production.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Focus on the final sentence: 'فقد تبين أن الوجود عند الشيء لا يدل على أنه موجود به.' This is the core principle Ghazali establishes.
Contrast 'وجود عند' (existence with) with 'وجود به' (existence by).
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why Ghazali makes this argument: to defend divine causality against attributing creative power to natural processes or human agents.
This connects to the broader Islamic position that Allah is the sole Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقوى المدركة والمحركة ، فى نطفة الحيوانات ، ليس يتولد عن الطبائع المحصورة في الحرارة والبرودة ، والرطوبة واليبوسة ، ولا أن الأب فاعل ابنه ، بإيداع النطفة في الرحم ، ولا هو فاعل حياته ، وبصره ، وسمعه ، وسائر المعاني التي هي فيه ، ومعلوم أنها موجودة عنده ، ولم يقل أحد ، إنها موجودة به ، بل وجودها من جهة الأول ، إما بغير واسطة ، وإما بواسطة الملائكة ، الموكلين بهذه الأمور الحادثة. وهذا مما يقطع به الفلاسفة القائلون بالصانع ، والكلام معهم. فقد تبين أن الوجود عند الشيء ، لا يدل على أنه موجود به.
The powers of perception and movement in the sperm of animals are not generated from the simple natures confined to heat, cold, moisture, and dryness; nor is the father the agent of his son by depositing the sperm in the womb, nor is he the agent of his life, sight, hearing, and all the other meanings that are in him. It is known that they exist with him (the father), but no one has said that they exist by him. Rather, their existence is from the First (al-Awwal), either without intermediary or through the angels appointed to these generated matters. This is something that the philosophers who affirm the Maker affirm, and the discussion is with them. So it has become clear that existence with a thing does not indicate that it exists by that thing.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the mere spatial or temporal coexistence of an effect with a cause does not entail that the effect is produced by that cause. Ghazali begins by observing that the faculties of sensation and movement in animal semen are not generated from the four elementary qualities. He then argues that the father, though he deposits the semen, is not the true agent of the child's life, sight, hearing, and other attributes; these are present with the father (i.e., in his offspring) but are not from him. Instead, they come from the First Principle, either directly or through angels. This is conceded by the philosophers who believe in a Creator. The conclusion (فقد تبين) is that the existence of something along with another thing does not prove that the former is caused by the latter.

Key Terms
القوى المدركة
القوى المحركة
الطبائع المحصورة
الملائكة الموكلون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The father is the creator of his son's life and abilities.
Correction: Ghazali argues that life, sight, and hearing are from Allah, not the father. The father is merely an intermediary.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, from where do the powers of sensation and movement in the animal drop originate?
A) From the elements of heat, cold, moisture, and dryness
B) From the father
C) From the First (Allah) directly or through angels
D) From the earth

Key Takeaway
The mere coexistence of an effect with a potential cause does not prove that the cause produced it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life, sight, hearing, and intellect exist with you right now. Are you truly convinced that they are not from you but from Allah? How would your day change if you consciously acknowledged this every moment?
?
Think of a personal achievement or relationship you often take pride in as 'yours.' According to this lesson, it only exists with you, not by you. Can you name one concrete outcome of attributing it entirely to Allah today?
?
If even the father is not the cause of his child's attributes, then every good thing in your life is directly from Allah. What is one blessing you have been treating as a natural consequence rather than a gift, and how will you return it to Allah in gratitude and reliance?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Blind Man Analogy: Revealing the Hidden Agents
Ghazali uses a blind man analogy to refute direct natural agency and affirm the Bestower of Forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the blind man analogy to show that observable causes are only preparations, while the true agent is the Bestower of Forms.

Core Idea
What we call causes are just preparations; the true agent is hidden.

Ghazali presents a powerful analogy to challenge the assumption that we directly observe causality. A blind man who gains sight initially thinks opening his eyes causes vision, but later learns sunlight is the true cause. Similarly, we mistake immediate physical events for ultimate causes. This lesson unpacks the analogy and its implications for natural agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: analogy, application, agreement of philosophers, refutation.
2
Understand the blind man analogy
Identify the stages: blind man with veil, removal, initial false inference, later correction after sunset. Understand how this illustrates hidden causality.
The analogy is not about blind people but about our epistemic situation.
3
Apply the analogy to natural events
Ghazali argues that we are like the blind man: we see regularities but miss the constant, hidden principles. The philosophers agree that these principles are the Bestower of Forms.
4
Recognize the refutation
The conclusion: fire, bread, medicine are not true agents. They are mere preparations. The true agent is the Bestower of Forms. This refutes the common view of direct natural agency.
This is a key point of occasionalist critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل نبين هذا بمثال ، وهو أن الأكمه ، لو كانت في عينيه غشاوة ، ولم يسمع من الناس ، الفرق بين الليل والنهار ، لو انكشفت الغشاوة عن عينيه نهاراً وفتح أجفانه ، فرأى الألوان ، ظن أن الإدراك الحاصل في عينيه ، لصور الألوان ، فاعله فتح البصر ، وأنه مهما كان بصره سليما ومفتوحاً ، والحجاب مرتفعاً ، والشخص المقابل متلوناً ، فيلزم لا محالة أن يبصر ، ولا يعقل أن لا يبصر؛ حتى إذا غربت الشمس ، وأظلم الهواء ، علم أن نور الشمس هو السبب ، في انطباع الألوان في بصره؛ فمن أين يأمن الخصم أن يكون في مبادئ الوجود ، علل وأسباب تفيض منها هذه الحوادث ، عند حصول ملاقاة بينها ؟! إلا أنها ثابتة ليست تنعدم ، ولا هي أجسام متحركة فتغيب؛ ولو انعدمت أو غابت لأدركنا التفرقة ، وفهمنا أن ثم سبباً وراء ما شاهدناه ، وهذا لا مخرج منه على قياس أصلهم. ولهذا اتفق محققوهم ، على أن هذه الأعراض والحوادث ، التي تحصل عند وقوع الملاقاة بين الأجسام. وعلى الجملة عند اختلاف نسبها ، إنما تفيض من عند واهب الصور ، وهو ملك من الملائكة ، حتى قالوا: انطباع صور الألوان في العين يحصل من جهة واهب الصور ، وإنما طلوع الشمس والحدقة السليمة ، والجسم المتلون ، معدات ومهيئات ، لقبول المحل هذه الصور ، وطردوا هذا في كل حادث. وبهذا تبطل دعوى من يدعى ، أن النار هي الفاعلة للإحراق ، والخبز هو الفاعل للشبع ، والدواء هو الفاعل للصحة ، إلى غير ذلك من الأسباب.
Rather, we will clarify this with an example: a blind man who has a veil over his eyes and has not heard from people the difference between night and day. If the veil were removed from his eyes during the day and he opened his eyelids, he would see colors. He would think that the perception occurring in his eyes of the forms of colors is caused by the opening of the sight, and that whenever his sight is sound and open, and the veil is lifted, and the opposite person is colored, then it necessarily follows that he sees, and it is inconceivable that he does not see. Until when the sun sets and the air becomes dark, he knows that sunlight is the cause of the impression of colors in his vision. So how can the opponent be sure that among the principles of existence there are causes and reasons from which these events flow when contact occurs between them? Except that they are constant, not non-existent, nor are they moving bodies that disappear; if they were non-existent or absent, we would perceive the difference and understand that there is a cause behind what we have witnessed. There is no way out of this according to the measure of their principles. For this reason, their learned (al-muḥaqqiqūn) have agreed that these accidents and events that occur when contact happens between bodies, and in general when their relations differ, only flow from the Bestower of Forms (Wāhib al-Ṣuwar), who is an angel from among the angels. They even said: the impression of the forms of colors in the eye occurs from the Bestower of Forms, and the rising of the sun, the sound eye, and the colored body are only instruments and preparations (muʿiddāt wa-muhayyiʾāt) for the receptacle to accept these forms. And they applied this to every event. And with this, the claim of those who claim that fire is the agent of burning, bread is the agent of satiety, medicine is the agent of health, and so on, is refuted.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy and a refutation of direct natural causation. Ghazali uses the example of a blind man whose veil is removed and who sees colors for the first time. He initially thinks that the act of opening his eyes is the cause of seeing. Only after sunset does he realize that sunlight is the true cause of color perception. Ghazali analogizes this to hidden metaphysical causes: we observe regularities but fail to see the constant, non-material principles from which events truly flow. These principles, the philosophers agree, are the Bestower of Forms (an angel). Observable factors like the sun, the eye, and the colored object are merely preparations (معدات ومهيئات). Thus, Ghazali refutes the common view that fire, bread, medicine, etc. are true agents; they are only occasions for the Bestower to act.

Key Terms
الأكمه
غشاوة
واهب الصور
معدات ومهيئات
مبادئ الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fire directly causes burning, bread directly causes satiety, medicine directly causes health.
Correction: These are only preparations; the true agent is the Bestower of Forms.

Checkpoint
What do the learned philosophers (محققوهم) say about the impression of colors in the eye?
A) It is caused by sunlight
B) It comes from the Bestower of Forms
C) It is a natural physical process
D) It is caused by the mind

Key Takeaway
Observable causes are only preparations; the true agent is the hidden Bestower of Forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed at something, do you genuinely believe that your efforts were only preparations, and that the Bestower of Forms directly granted the outcome? How does this belief change the way you celebrate or feel pride?
?
Think of a recent blessing you took for granted—like a meal that satisfied you or a recovery from illness. Did you silently attribute it to the food or medicine, or did you remember that the true agent is hidden? What would it take for you to pause and thank the Bestower in that moment?
?
If you truly internalized that no object or human has independent causal power, which relationship or habit would you need to re-evaluate? To whom or what do you instinctively assign agency—and how can you train your heart to look past the preparation to the real Giver?
